id="mw-head-base" class="noprint"></div>/n		<div id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">/n			<a id="top"></a>/n/n							<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div>/n						<div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">/n</div>/n			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Supercomputer</h1>/n									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">/n									<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>/n								<div id="contentSub"></div>/n												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">/n					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>/n				</div>/n				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"High-performance computing" redirects here. For narrower definitions of HPC, see <a href="/wiki/High-throughput_computing" title="High-throughput computing">High-throughput computing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Many-task_computing" title="Many-task computing">Many-task computing</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Supercomputer (disambiguation)">Supercomputer (disambiguation)</a>.</div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IBM_Blue_Gene_P_supercomputer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/IBM_Blue_Gene_P_supercomputer.jpg/300px-IBM_Blue_Gene_P_supercomputer.jpg" width="300" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/IBM_Blue_Gene_P_supercomputer.jpg/450px-IBM_Blue_Gene_P_supercomputer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/IBM_Blue_Gene_P_supercomputer.jpg/600px-IBM_Blue_Gene_P_supercomputer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2100" data-file-height="1391" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IBM_Blue_Gene_P_supercomputer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nThe <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> <a href="/wiki/Blue_Gene" title="Blue Gene">Blue Gene/P</a> supercomputer "Intrepid" at <a href="/wiki/Argonne_National_Laboratory" title="Argonne National Laboratory">Argonne National Laboratory</a> runs 164,000 processor cores using normal data center air conditioning, grouped in 40 racks/cabinets connected by a high-speed 3-D torus network.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>A <b>supercomputer</b> is a computer with a high level of computing performance compared to a <a href="/wiki/General-purpose_computer" class="mw-redirect" title="General-purpose computer">general-purpose computer</a>. Performance of a supercomputer is measured in <a href="/wiki/Floating-point" class="mw-redirect" title="Floating-point">floating-point</a> operations per second (<a href="/wiki/FLOPS" title="FLOPS">FLOPS</a>) instead of <a href="/wiki/Million_instructions_per_second" class="mw-redirect" title="Million instructions per second">million instructions per second</a> (MIPS). As of 2017, there are supercomputers which can perform up to nearly a hundred <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(numbers)#1015" title="Orders of magnitude (numbers)">quadrillions</a> of FLOPS<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>, measured in P(eta)FLOPS.<sup id="cite_ref-November_2014_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-November_2014-4">[4]</a></sup> The majority of supercomputers today run <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>-based operating systems.</p>/n<p>Supercomputers play an important role in the field of <a href="/wiki/Computational_science" title="Computational science">computational science</a>, and are used for a wide range of computationally intensive tasks in various fields, including <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weather_forecasting" title="Weather forecasting">weather forecasting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_research" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate research">climate research</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oil_and_gas_exploration" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil and gas exploration">oil and gas exploration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computational_chemistry" title="Computational chemistry">molecular modeling</a> (computing the structures and properties of chemical compounds, biological <a href="/wiki/Macromolecules" class="mw-redirect" title="Macromolecules">macromolecules</a>, polymers, and crystals), and physical simulations (such as simulations of the early moments of the universe, airplane and spacecraft aerodynamics, the detonation of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>). Throughout their history, they have been essential in the field of <a href="/wiki/Cryptanalysis" title="Cryptanalysis">cryptanalysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Supercomputers were introduced in the 1960s, and for several decades the fastest were made by <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Cray" title="Seymour Cray">Seymour Cray</a> at <a href="/wiki/Control_Data_Corporation" title="Control Data Corporation">Control Data Corporation</a> (CDC), <a href="/wiki/Cray_Research" class="mw-redirect" title="Cray Research">Cray Research</a> and subsequent companies bearing his name or monogram. The first such machines were highly tuned conventional designs that ran faster than their more general-purpose contemporaries. Through the 1960s, they began to add increasing amounts of <a href="/wiki/Parallel_computing" title="Parallel computing">parallelism</a> with one to four <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">processors</a> being typical. From the 1970s, the <a href="/wiki/Vector_processor" title="Vector processor">vector computing</a> concept with specialized math units operating on large arrays of data came to dominate. A notable example is the highly successful <a href="/wiki/Cray-1" title="Cray-1">Cray-1</a> of 1976. Vector computers remained the dominant design into the 1990s. From then until today, <a href="/wiki/Massively_parallel_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Massively parallel (computing)">massively parallel</a> supercomputers with tens of thousands of off-the-shelf processors became the norm.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoffman_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jouppi_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jouppi-7">[7]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The US has long been a leader in the supercomputer field, first through Cray's almost uninterrupted dominance of the field, and later through a variety of technology companies. Japan made major strides in the field in the 1980s and 90s, but since then China has become increasingly important. As of June 2016, the fastest supercomputer on the <a href="/wiki/TOP500" title="TOP500">TOP500</a> supercomputer list is the <a href="/wiki/Sunway_TaihuLight" title="Sunway TaihuLight">Sunway TaihuLight</a>, in China, with a <a href="/wiki/LINPACK_benchmark" class="mw-redirect" title="LINPACK benchmark">LINPACK benchmark</a> score of 93&#160;PFLOPS, exceeding the previous record holder, <a href="/wiki/Tianhe-2" title="Tianhe-2">Tianhe-2</a>, by around 59&#160;PFLOPS. Sunway TaihuLight's emergence is also notable for its use of indigenous chips, and is the first Chinese computer to enter the TOP500 list without using hardware from the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. As of June 2016, China, for the first time, had more computers (167) on the TOP500 list than the United States (165). However, US built computers held ten of the top 20 positions;<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> in November 2016 the U.S. has five of the top 10 and China two, in fact the top two.</p>/n<p></p>/n<div id="toc" class="toc">/n<div list</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Largest_Supercomputer_Vendors_according_to_the_total_Rmax_.28GFLOPS.29_operated"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> tocsection-23"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Notes_and_references"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Notes and references</span></a></li>/n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>/n</ul>/n</div>/n<p></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_supercomputing" title="History of supercomputing">History of supercomputing</a></div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cray-1-deutsches-museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Cray-1-deutsches-museum.jpg/220px-Cray-1-deutsches-museum.jpg" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Cray-1-deutsches-museum.jpg/330px-Cray-1-deutsches-museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Cray-1-deutsches-museum.jpg/440px-Cray-1-deutsches-museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1045" data-file-height="1151" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cray-1-deutsches-museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nA <a href="/wiki/Cray-1" title="Cray-1">Cray-1</a> preserved at the <a href="/wiki/Deutsches_Museum" title="Deutsches Museum">Deutsches Museum</a></div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>The history of supercomputing goes back to the 1960s, with the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(computer)" title="Atlas (computer)">Atlas</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Victoria_University_of_Manchester" title="Victoria University of Manchester">University of Manchester</a> and a series of computers at <a href="/wiki/Control_Data_Corporation" title="Control Data Corporation">Control Data Corporation</a> (CDC), designed by <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Cray" title="Seymour Cray">Seymour Cray</a>. These used innovative designs and parallelism to achieve superior computational peak performance.<sup id="cite_ref-chen_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chen-10">[10]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(computer)" title="Atlas (computer)">Atlas</a> was a joint venture between <a href="/wiki/Ferranti" title="Ferranti">Ferranti</a> and the Manchester University and was designed to operate at processing speeds approaching one&#160;microsecond per instruction, about one&#160;million instructions per second.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The first Atlas was officially commissioned on 7 December 1962 as one of the world's first supercomputers &#160;â€“  considered to be the most powerful computer in the world at that time by a considerable margin, and equivalent to four <a href="/wiki/IBM_7094" class="mw-redirect" title="IBM 7094">IBM 7094s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>/n<p>For the <a href="/wiki/CDC_6600" title="CDC 6600">CDC 6600</a> (which Cray designed) released in 1964, a switch from using germanium to silicon transistors was implemented, as they could run very fast, solving the overheating problem by introducing refrigeration,<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and helped to make it the fastest in the world. Given that the 6600 outperformed all the other contemporary computers by about 10 times, it was dubbed a <i>supercomputer</i> and defined the supercomputing market, when one hundred computers were sold at $8 million each.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hannan_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hannan-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Cray left CDC in 1972 to form his own company, <a href="/wiki/Cray" title="Cray">Cray Research</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hannan_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hannan-15">[15]</a></sup> Four years after leaving CDC, Cray delivered the 80&#160;MHz <a href="/wiki/Cray_1" class="mw-redirect" title="Cray 1">Cray 1</a> in 1976, and it became one of the most successful supercomputers in history.<sup id="cite_ref-Hill41_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hill41-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Edwin65_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwin65-19">[19]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cray-2" title="Cray-2">Cray-2</a> released in 1985 was an 8 processor <a href="/wiki/Computer_cooling" title="Computer cooling">liquid cooled</a> computer and <a href="/wiki/Fluorinert" title="Fluorinert">Fluorinert</a> was pumped through it as it operated. It performed at 1.9 <a href="/wiki/GigaFLOPS" class="mw-redirect" title="GigaFLOPS">gigaFLOPS</a> and was the world's second fastest after M-13 supercomputer in Moscow .<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In 1982, <a href="/wiki/Osaka_University" title="Osaka University">Osaka University</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Supercomputing_in_Japan" title="Supercomputing in Japan">LINKS-1 Computer Graphics System</a> used a <a href="/wiki/Massively_parallel" title="Massively parallel">massively parallel</a> processing architecture, with 514 <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessors</a>, including 257 <a href="/wiki/Zilog_Z8000" title="Zilog Z8000">Zilog Z8001</a> <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">control processors</a> and 257 <a href="/wiki/IAPX" title="IAPX">iAPX</a> <a href="/wiki/IAPX_86" class="mw-redirect" title="IAPX 86">86/20</a> <a href="/wiki/Floating-point_unit" title="Floating-point unit">floating-point processors</a>. It was mainly used for rendering realistic <a href="/wiki/3D_computer_graphics" title="3D computer graphics">3D</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_graphics" title="Computer graphics">computer graphics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>/n<p>While the supercomputers of the 1980s used only a few processors, in the 1990s, machines with thousands of processors began to <a href="/wiki/Supercomputing_in_Japan" title="Supercomputing in Japan">appear in Japan</a> and the United States, setting new computational performance records. <a href="/wiki/Fujitsu" title="Fujitsu">Fujitsu</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Numerical_Wind_Tunnel" class="mw-redirect" title="Numerical Wind Tunnel">Numerical Wind Tunnel</a> supercomputer used 166 vector processors to gain the top spot in 1994 with a peak speed of 1.7&#160;<a href="/wiki/FLOPS" title="FLOPS">gigaFLOPS (GFLOPS)</a> per processor.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hitachi_SR2201" title="Hitachi SR2201">Hitachi SR2201</a> obtained a peak performance of 600&#160;GFLOPS in 1996 by using 2048 processors connected via a fast three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Crossbar_switch" title="Crossbar switch">crossbar</a> network.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Intel_Paragon" title="Intel Paragon">Intel Paragon</a> could have 1000 to 4000 <a href="/wiki/Intel_i860" title="Intel i860">Intel i860</a> processors in various configurations, and was ranked the fastest in the world in 1993. The Paragon was a <a href="/wiki/MIMD" title="MIMD">MIMD</a> machine which connected processors via a high speed two <a href="/wiki/Crossbar_switch" title="Crossbar switch">dimensional mesh</a>, allowing processes to execute on separate nodes, communicating via the <a href="/wiki/Message_Passing_Interface" title="Message Passing Interface">Message Passing Interface</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hardware_and_architecture">Hardware and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Hardware and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer_architecture" title="Supercomputer architecture">Supercomputer architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parallel_computer_hardware" class="mw-redirect" title="Parallel computer hardware">Parallel computer hardware</a></div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BlueGeneL_cabinet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/BlueGeneL_cabinet.jpg/170px-BlueGeneL_cabinet.jpg" width="170" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/BlueGeneL_cabinet.jpg/255px-BlueGeneL_cabinet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/BlueGeneL_cabinet.jpg/340px-BlueGeneL_cabinet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1244" data-file-height="1906" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BlueGeneL_cabinet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nA <a href="/wiki/Blue_Gene" title="Blue Gene">Blue Gene</a>/L cabinet showing the stacked <a href="/wiki/Blade_server" title="Blade server">blades</a>, each holding many processors</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Approaches to <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer_architecture" title="Supercomputer architecture">supercomputer architecture</a> have taken dramatic turns since the earliest systems were introduced in the 1960s.</p>/n<h3><span id="Early_machines;_simple_but_fast"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_machines.3B_simple_but_fast">Early machines; simple but fast</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early machines; simple but fast">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Early supercomputer architectures pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Seymour_Cray" title="Seymour Cray">Seymour Cray</a> relied on compact designs and local <a href="/wiki/Parallel_computing" title="Parallel computing">parallelism</a> to achieve superior computational performance.<sup id="cite_ref-chen_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chen-10">[10]</a></sup> Cray had noted that increasing processor speeds did little if the rest of the system did not also improve; the CPU would end up waiting longer for data to arrive from the offboard storage units. The <a href="/wiki/CDC_6600" title="CDC 6600">CDC 6600</a>, the first mass-produced supercomputer, solved this problem by providing ten simple computers whose only purpose was to read and write data to and from <a href="/wiki/Main_memory" class="mw-redirect" title="Main memory">main memory</a>, allowing the CPU to concentrate solely on processing the data. This made both the main CPU and the ten "PPU" units much simpler. As such, they were physically smaller and reduced the amount of wiring between the various parts. This reduced the electrical signaling delays and allowed the system to run at a higher clock speed. The 6600 outperformed all other machines by an average of 10 times when it was introduced.</p>/n<p>The CDC 6600's spot as the fastest computer was eventually replaced by its successor, the <a href="/wiki/CDC_7600" title="CDC 7600">CDC 7600</a>. This design was very similar to the 6600 in general organization but added <a href="/wiki/Instruction_pipelining" title="Instruction pipelining">instruction pipelining</a> to further improve performance. Generally speaking, every <a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_instruction&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Computer instruction (page does not exist)">computer instruction</a> required several steps to process; first, the instruction is read from memory, then any required data it refers to is read, the instruction is processed, and the results are written back out to memory. Each of these steps is normally accomplished by separate circuitry. In most early computers, including the 6600, each of these steps runs in turn, and while any one unit is currently active, the hardware handling the other parts of the process is idle. In the 7600, as soon as one instruction cleared a particular unit, that unit began processing the next instruction. Although each instruction takes the same time to complete, there are parts of several instructions being processed at the same time, offering much-improved overall performance. This, combined with further packaging improvements and improvements in the electronics, made the 7600 about four to ten times as fast as the 6600.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vector_designs">Vector designs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vector designs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>The 7600 was intended to be replaced by the <a href="/wiki/CDC_8600" title="CDC 8600">CDC 8600</a>, which was essentially four 7600's in a small box. However, this design ran into intractable problems and was eventually canceled in 1974 in favor of another CDC design, the <a href="/wiki/CDC_STAR-100" title="CDC STAR-100">CDC STAR-100</a>. The STAR was essentially a simplified and slower version of the 7600, but it was combined with new circuits that could rapidly process sequences of math instructions. The basic idea was similar to the pipeline in the 7600 but geared entirely toward math, and in theory, much faster. In practice, the STAR proved to have poor real-world performance, and ultimately only two or three were built.</p>/n<p>Cray, meanwhile, had left CDC and formed his own company. Considering the problems with the STAR, he designed an improved version of the same basic concept but replaced the STAR's memory-based vectors with ones that ran in large <a href="/w/index.php?title=Computer_register&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Computer register (page does not exist)">registers</a>. Combining this with his famous packaging improvements produced the <a href="/wiki/Cray-1" title="Cray-1">Cray-1</a>. This completely outperformed every computer in the world, save one, and would ultimately sell about 80 units, making it one of the most successful supercomputer systems in history. Through the 1970s, 80s and 90s a series of machines from Cray further improved on these basic concepts.</p>/n<p>The basic concept of using a pipeline dedicated to processing large data units became known as <a href="/wiki/Vector_processing" class="mw-redirect" title="Vector processing">vector processing</a>, and came to dominate the supercomputer field. A number of Japanese firms also entered the field, producing similar concepts in much smaller machines. Three main lines were produced by these companies, the <a href="/wiki/Fujitsu_VP" title="Fujitsu VP">Fujitsu VP</a>, <a href="/wiki/HITAC_S-810" title="HITAC S-810">Hitachi HITAC</a> and <a href="/wiki/NEC_SX_architecture" title="NEC SX architecture">NEC SX</a> series, all announced in the early 1980s and updated continually into the 1990s. CDC attempted to re-enter this market with the <a href="/wiki/ETA10" title="ETA10">ETA10</a> but this was not very successful. <a href="/wiki/Convex_Computer" title="Convex Computer">Convex Computer</a> took another route, introducing a series of much smaller vector machines aimed at smaller businesses.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Massively_parallel_designs">Massively parallel designs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Massively parallel designs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>The only computer to seriously challenge the Cray-1's performance in the 1970s was the <a href="/wiki/ILLIAC_IV" title="ILLIAC IV">ILLIAC IV</a>. This machine was the first realized example of a true <a href="/wiki/Massively_parallel" title="Massively parallel">massively parallel</a> computer, in which many processors worked together to solve different parts of a single larger problem. In contrast with the vector systems, which were designed to run a single stream of data as quickly as possible, in this concept, the computer instead feeds separate parts of the data to entirely different processors and then recombines the results. The ILLIAC's design was finalized in 1966 with 256 processors and offer speed up to 1 GFLOPS, compared to the 1970s Cray-1's peak of 250 MFLOPS. However, development problems led to only 64 processors being built, and the system could never operate faster than about 200 MFLOPS, while being much larger and more complex than the Cray. Another problem was that writing software for the system was difficult, and getting peak performance from it was a matter of serious effort.</p>/n<p>But the partial success of the ILLIAC IV was widely seen as pointing the way to the future of supercomputing. Cray argued against this, famously quipping that "If you were plowing a field, which would you rather use? Two strong oxen or 1024 chickens?"<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> But by the early 1980s, several teams were working on parallel designs with thousands of processors, notably the <a href="/wiki/Connection_Machine" title="Connection Machine">Connection Machine</a> (CM) that developed from research at <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a>. The CM-1 used as many as 65,536 simplified custom <a href="/wiki/Microprocessor" title="Microprocessor">microprocessors</a> connected together in a <a href="/wiki/Computer_network" title="Computer network">network</a> to share data. Several updated versions followed; the CM-5 supercomputer is a massively parallel processing computer capable of many billions of arithmetic operations per second.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Software development remained a problem, but the CM series sparked off considerable research into this issue. Similar designs using custom hardware were made by many companies, including the <a href="/wiki/Evans_%26_Sutherland_ES-1" title="Evans &amp; Sutherland ES-1">Evans &amp; Sutherland ES-1</a>, <a href="/wiki/MasPar" title="MasPar">MasPar</a>, <a href="/wiki/NCUBE" title="NCUBE">nCUBE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Intel_iPSC" title="Intel iPSC">Intel iPSC</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Goodyear_MPP" title="Goodyear MPP">Goodyear MPP</a>. But by the mid-1990s, general-purpose CPU performance had improved so much in that a supercomputer could be built using them as the individual processing units, instead of using custom chips. By the turn of the <a href="/wiki/21st_century" title="21st century">21st century</a>, designs featuring tens of thousands of commodity CPUs were the norm, with later machines adding <a href="/wiki/GPGPU" class="mw-redirect" title="GPGPU">graphic units</a>) to the mix.<sup id="cite_ref-Hoffman_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Jouppi_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jouppi-7">[7]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Throughout the decades, the management of <a href="/wiki/Heat_density" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat density">heat density</a> has remained a key issue for most centralized supercomputers.<sup id="cite_ref-TH1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TH1-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Charley_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Charley-31">[31]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rupak_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rupak-32">[32]</a></sup> The large amount of heat generated by a system may also have other effects, e.g. reducing the lifetime of other system components.<sup id="cite_ref-Huang313_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Huang313-33">[33]</a></sup> There have been diverse approaches to heat management, from pumping <a href="/wiki/Fluorinert" title="Fluorinert">Fluorinert</a> through the system, to a hybrid liquid-air cooling system or air cooling with normal <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Tokhi_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokhi-34">[34]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sysx_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sysx-35">[35]</a></sup></p>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Processor_families_in_TOP500_supercomputers.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Processor_families_in_TOP500_supercomputers.svg/220px-Processor_families_in_TOP500_supercomputers.svg.png" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Processor_families_in_TOP500_supercomputers.svg/330px-Processor_families_in_TOP500_supercomputers.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Processor_families_in_TOP500_supercomputers.svg/440px-Processor_families_in_TOP500_supercomputers.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1179" data-file-height="838" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Processor_families_in_TOP500_supercomputers.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nThe CPU share of <a href="/wiki/TOP500" title="TOP500">TOP500</a></div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Systems with a massive number of processors generally take one of two paths. In the <a href="/wiki/Grid_computing" title="Grid computing">grid computing</a> approach, the processing power of many computers, organised as distributed, diverse administrative domains, is opportunistically used whenever a computer is available.<sup id="cite_ref-Prodan_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prodan-36">[36]</a></sup> In another approach, a large number of processors are used in proximity to each other, e.g. in a <a href="/wiki/Computer_cluster" title="Computer cluster">computer cluster</a>. In such a centralized <a href="/wiki/Massively_parallel" title="Massively parallel">massively parallel</a> system the speed and flexibility of the <i><span id="interconnect">interconnect</span></i> becomes very important and modern supercomputers have used various approaches ranging from enhanced <a href="/wiki/Infiniband" class="mw-redirect" title="Infiniband">Infiniband</a> systems to three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Torus_interconnect" title="Torus interconnect">torus interconnects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bluenight_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bluenight-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The use of <a href="/wiki/Multi-core_processor" title="Multi-core processor">multi-core processors</a> combined with centralization is an emerging direction, e.g. as in the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops64" title="Cyclops64">Cyclops64</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-Cellular_Computer_Architecture_Cyclops64.27_2005.2C_pages_132.E2.80.93143_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cellular_Computer_Architecture_Cyclops64.27_2005.2C_pages_132.E2.80.93143-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guangming_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guangming-40">[40]</a></sup></p>/n<p>As the price, performance and energy efficiency of <a href="/wiki/GPGPU" class="mw-redirect" title="GPGPU">general purpose graphic processors</a> (GPGPUs) have improved,<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> a number of <a href="/wiki/PetaFLOPS" class="mw-redirect" title="PetaFLOPS">petaFLOPS</a> supercomputers such as <a href="/wiki/Tianhe-I" title="Tianhe-I">Tianhe-I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nebulae_(computer)" title="Nebulae (computer)">Nebulae</a> have started to rely on them.<sup id="cite_ref-GPGPU_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GPGPU-42">[42]</a></sup> However, other systems such as the <a href="/wiki/K_computer" title="K computer">K computer</a> continue to use conventional processors such as <a href="/wiki/SPARC" title="SPARC">SPARC</a>-based designs and the overall applicability of <a href="/wiki/GPGPU" class="mw-redirect" title="GPGPU">GPGPUs</a> in general-purpose high-performance computing applications has been the subject of debate, in that while a GPGPU may be tuned to score well on specific benchmarks, its overall applicability to everyday algorithms may be limited unless significant effort is spent to tune the application towards it.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HansH_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HansH-44">[44]</a></sup> However, GPUs are gaining ground and in 2012 the <a href="/wiki/Jaguar_supercomputer" class="mw-redirect" title="Jaguar supercomputer">Jaguar supercomputer</a> was transformed into <a href="/wiki/Titan_(supercomputer)" title="Titan (supercomputer)">Titan</a> by retrofitting CPUs with GPUs.<sup id="cite_ref-PC_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PC-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TitanReg_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TitanReg-47">[47]</a></sup></p>/n<p>High performance computers have an expected life cycle of about three years before requiring an upgrade.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>/n<p>A number of "special-purpose" systems have been designed, dedicated to a single problem. This allows the use of specially programmed <a href="/wiki/Field-programmable_gate_array" title="Field-programmable gate array">FPGA</a> chips or even custom <a href="/wiki/Application-specific_integrated_circuit" title="Application-specific integrated circuit">ASICs</a>, allowing better price/performance ratios by sacrificing generality. Examples of special-purpose supercomputers include <a href="/wiki/Belle_(chess_machine)" title="Belle (chess machine)">Belle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/IBM_Deep_Blue" class="mw-redirect" title="IBM Deep Blue">Deep Blue</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(chess)" title="Hydra (chess)">Hydra</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> for playing <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Pipe" title="Gravity Pipe">Gravity Pipe</a> for astrophysics,<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/MDGRAPE-3" class="mw-redirect" title="MDGRAPE-3">MDGRAPE-3</a> for protein structure computation molecular dynamics<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Deep_Crack" class="mw-redirect" title="Deep Crack">Deep Crack</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> for breaking the <a href="/wiki/Data_Encryption_Standard" title="Data Encryption Standard">DES</a> <a href="/wiki/Cipher" title="Cipher">cipher</a>.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_usage_and_heat_management">Energy usage and heat management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Energy usage and heat management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Computer_cooling" title="Computer cooling">Computer cooling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_500" class="mw-redirect" title="Green 500">Green 500</a></div>/n<p>A typical supercomputer consumes large amounts of electrical power, almost all of which is converted into heat, requiring cooling. For example, <a href="/wiki/Tianhe-1A" class="mw-redirect" title="Tianhe-1A">Tianhe-1A</a> consumes 4.04&#160;<a href="/wiki/Megawatt" class="mw-redirect" title="Megawatt">megawatts</a> (MW) of electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The cost to power and cool the system can be significant, e.g. 4&#160;MW at $0.10/kWh is $400 an hour or about $3.5 million per year.</p>/n<div class="thumb tleft">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IBM_HS20_blade_server.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/IBM_HS20_blade_server.jpg/220px-IBM_HS20_blade_server.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/IBM_HS20_blade_server.jpg/330px-IBM_HS20_blade_server.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/IBM_HS20_blade_server.jpg/440px-IBM_HS20_blade_server.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IBM_HS20_blade_server.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nAn <a href="/wiki/IBM_BladeCenter#HS20" title="IBM BladeCenter">IBM HS20</a> <a href="/wiki/Blade_server" title="Blade server">blade</a></div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Heat management is a major issue in complex electronic devices and affects powerful computer systems in various ways.<sup id="cite_ref-Spectrum_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spectrum-56">[56]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Thermal_design_power" title="Thermal design power">thermal design power</a> and <a href="/wiki/CPU_power_dissipation" title="CPU power dissipation">CPU power dissipation</a> issues in supercomputing surpass those of traditional <a href="/wiki/Computer_cooling" title="Computer cooling">computer cooling</a> technologies. The supercomputing awards for <a href="/wiki/Green_computing" title="Green computing">green computing</a> reflect this issue.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WuFeng_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WuFeng-59">[59]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The packing of thousands of processors together inevitably generates significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Heat_density" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat density">heat density</a> that need to be dealt with. The <a href="/wiki/Cray_2" class="mw-redirect" title="Cray 2">Cray 2</a> was <a href="/wiki/Computer_cooling" title="Computer cooling">liquid cooled</a>, and used a <a href="/wiki/Fluorinert" title="Fluorinert">Fluorinert</a> "cooling waterfall" which was forced through the modules under pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-Tokhi_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tokhi-34">[34]</a></sup> However, the submerged liquid cooling approach was not practical for the multi-cabinet systems based on off-the-shelf processors, and in <a href="/wiki/System_X_(computing)" title="System X (computing)">System X</a> a special cooling system that combined air conditioning with liquid cooling was developed in conjunction with the <a href="/wiki/Liebert_(company)" title="Liebert (company)">Liebert company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sysx_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sysx-35">[35]</a></sup></p>/n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Gene" title="Blue Gene">Blue Gene</a> system, IBM deliberately used low power processors to deal with heat density.<sup id="cite_ref-TheRegSC10_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TheRegSC10-60">[60]</a></sup> The IBM <a href="/wiki/Power_775" title="Power 775">Power 775</a>, released in 2011, has closely packed elements that require water cooling.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The IBM <a href="/wiki/Aquasar" title="Aquasar">Aquasar</a> system uses <i>hot water cooling</i> to achieve energy efficiency, the water being used to heat buildings as well.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The energy efficiency of computer systems is generally measured in terms of "FLOPS per <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watt</a>". In 2008, <a href="/wiki/IBM_Roadrunner" title="IBM Roadrunner">IBM's Roadrunner</a> operated at 3.76&#160;<a href="/wiki/Performance_per_watt" title="Performance per watt">MFLOPS/W</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In November 2010, the <a href="/wiki/IBM_Blue_Gene#Blue_Gene.2FQ" class="mw-redirect" title="IBM Blue Gene">Blue Gene/Q</a> reached 1,684&#160;MFLOPS/W.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> In June 2011 the top 2 spots on the <a href="/wiki/Green_500" class="mw-redirect" title="Green 500">Green 500</a> list were occupied by <a href="/wiki/Blue_Gene" title="Blue Gene">Blue Gene</a> machines in New York (one achieving 2097&#160;MFLOPS/W) with the <a href="/wiki/DEGIMA_(computer_cluster)" class="mw-redirect" title="DEGIMA (computer cluster)">DEGIMA cluster</a> in Nagasaki placing third with 1375&#160;MFLOPS/W.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Because copper wires can transfer energy into a supercomputer with much higher power densities than forced air or circulating refrigerants can remove waste heat,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> the ability of the cooling systems to remove waste heat is a limiting factor.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, many existing supercomputers have more infrastructure capacity than the actual peak demand of the machine &#160;â€“  designers generally conservatively design the power and cooling infrastructure to handle more than the theoretical peak electrical power consumed by the supercomputer. Designs for future supercomputers are power-limited &#160;â€“  the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_design_power" title="Thermal design power">thermal design power</a> of the supercomputer as a whole, the amount that the power and cooling infrastructure can handle, is somewhat more than the expected normal power consumption, but less than the theoretical peak power consumption of the electronic hardware.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>/n<p><br /></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Software_and_system_management">Software and system management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Software and system management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operating_systems">Operating systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Operating systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer_operating_systems" title="Supercomputer operating systems">Supercomputer operating systems</a></div>/n<p>Since the end of the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer_operating_systems" title="Supercomputer operating systems">supercomputer operating systems</a> have undergone major transformations, based on the changes in <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer_architecture" title="Supercomputer architecture">supercomputer architecture</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Padua426_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padua426-73">[73]</a></sup> While early operating systems were custom tailored to each supercomputer to gain speed, the trend has been to move away from in-house operating systems to the adaptation of generic software such as <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MacKenzie_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacKenzie-74">[74]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Since modern <a href="/wiki/Massively_parallel" title="Massively parallel">massively parallel</a> supercomputers typically separate computations from other services by using multiple types of <a href="/wiki/Locale_(computer_hardware)" title="Locale (computer hardware)">nodes</a>, they usually run different operating systems on different nodes, e.g. using a small and efficient <a href="/wiki/Lightweight_Kernel_Operating_System" title="Lightweight Kernel Operating System">lightweight kernel</a> such as <a href="/wiki/CNK_operating_system" title="CNK operating system">CNK</a> or <a href="/wiki/Compute_Node_Linux" title="Compute Node Linux">CNL</a> on compute nodes, but a larger system such as a <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a>-derivative on server and <a href="/wiki/I/O" class="mw-redirect" title="I/O">I/O</a> nodes.<sup id="cite_ref-EuroPar2004_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EuroPar2004-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EuroPar2006_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EuroPar2006-76">[76]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Alam_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alam-77">[77]</a></sup></p>/n<p>While in a traditional multi-user computer system <a href="/wiki/Job_scheduling" class="mw-redirect" title="Job scheduling">job scheduling</a> is, in effect, a <a href="/wiki/Task_scheduling" class="mw-redirect" title="Task scheduling">tasking</a> problem for processing and peripheral resources, in a massively parallel system, the job management system needs to manage the allocation of both computational and communication resources, as well as gracefully deal with inevitable hardware failures when tens of thousands of processors are present.<sup id="cite_ref-Yariv_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yariv-78">[78]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Although most modern supercomputers use the <a href="/wiki/Linux" title="Linux">Linux</a> operating system, each manufacturer has its own specific Linux-derivative, and no industry standard exists, partly due to the fact that the differences in hardware architectures require changes to optimize the operating system to each hardware design.<sup id="cite_ref-Padua426_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padua426-73">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Software_tools_and_message_passing">Software tools and message passing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Software tools and message passing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Message_passing_in_computer_clusters" title="Message passing in computer clusters">Message passing in computer clusters</a></div>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Parallel_computing" title="Parallel computing">Parallel computing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parallel_programming_model" title="Parallel programming model">Parallel programming model</a></div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wide-angle_view_of_the_ALMA_correlator.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Wide-angle_view_of_the_ALMA_correlator.jpg/220px-Wide-angle_view_of_the_ALMA_correlator.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Wide-angle_view_of_the_ALMA_correlator.jpg/330px-Wide-angle_view_of_the_ALMA_correlator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Wide-angle_view_of_the_ALMA_correlator.jpg/440px-Wide-angle_view_of_the_ALMA_correlator.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2647" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wide-angle_view_of_the_ALMA_correlator.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nWide-angle view of the <a href="/wiki/Atacama_Large_Millimeter_Array" title="Atacama Large Millimeter Array">ALMA</a> correlator.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>The parallel architectures of supercomputers often dictate the use of special programming techniques to exploit their speed. Software tools for distributed processing include standard <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" title="Application programming interface">APIs</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Message_Passing_Interface" title="Message Passing Interface">MPI</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parallel_Virtual_Machine" title="Parallel Virtual Machine">PVM</a>, <a href="/wiki/Virtual_tape_library" title="Virtual tape library">VTL</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Open_source" class="mw-redirect" title="Open source">open source</a>-based software solutions such as <a href="/wiki/Beowulf_(computing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Beowulf (computing)">Beowulf</a>.</p>/n<p>In the most common scenario, environments such as <a href="/wiki/Parallel_Virtual_Machine" title="Parallel Virtual Machine">PVM</a> and <a href="/wiki/Message_Passing_Interface" title="Message Passing Interface">MPI</a> for loosely connected clusters and <a href="/wiki/OpenMP" title="OpenMP">OpenMP</a> for tightly coordinated shared memory machines are used. Significant effort is required to optimize an algorithm for the interconnect characteristics of the machine it will be run on; the aim is to prevent any of the CPUs from wasting time waiting on data from other nodes. <a href="/wiki/GPGPU" class="mw-redirect" title="GPGPU">GPGPUs</a> have hundreds of processor cores and are programmed using programming models such as <a href="/wiki/CUDA" title="CUDA">CUDA</a> or <a href="/wiki/OpenCL" title="OpenCL">OpenCL</a>.</p>/n<p>Moreover, it is quite difficult to debug and test parallel programs. <a href="/wiki/Testing_high-performance_computing_applications" title="Testing high-performance computing applications">Special techniques</a> need to be used for testing and debugging such applications.</p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distributed_supercomputing">Distributed supercomputing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Distributed supercomputing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opportunistic_approaches">Opportunistic approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Opportunistic approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grid_computing" title="Grid computing">Grid computing</a></div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ArchitectureCloudLinksSameSite.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/ArchitectureCloudLinksSameSite.png/220px-ArchitectureCloudLinksSameSite.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/ArchitectureCloudLinksSameSite.png 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="225" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ArchitectureCloudLinksSameSite.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nExample architecture of a <a href="/wiki/Grid_computing" title="Grid computing">grid computing</a> system connecting many personal computers over the internet</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Opportunistic Supercomputing is a form of networked <a href="/wiki/Grid_computing" title="Grid computing">grid computing</a> whereby a "super virtual computer" of many <a href="/wiki/Loose_coupling" title="Loose coupling">loosely coupled</a> volunteer computing machines performs very large computing tasks. Grid computing has been applied to a number of large-scale <a href="/wiki/Embarrassingly_parallel" title="Embarrassingly parallel">embarrassingly parallel</a> problems that require supercomputing performance scales. However, basic grid and <a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing" title="Cloud computing">cloud computing</a> approaches that rely on <a href="/wiki/Volunteer_computing" title="Volunteer computing">volunteer computing</a> can not handle traditional supercomputing tasks such as fluid dynamic simulations.</p>/n<p>The fastest grid computing system is the <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing_project" class="mw-redirect" title="Distributed computing project">distributed computing project</a> <a href="/wiki/Folding@home" title="Folding@home">Folding@home</a>. F@h reported 101 PFLOPS of <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a> processing power As of October 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>. Of this, over 100 PFLOPS are contributed by clients running on various GPUs, and the rest from various CPU systems.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>/n<p>The <a href="/wiki/BOINC" class="mw-redirect" title="BOINC">BOINC</a> platform hosts a number of distributed computing projects. As of February 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, BOINC recorded a processing power of over 166 PetaFLOPS through over 762 thousand active Computers (Hosts) on the network.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>/n<p>As of October 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Great_Internet_Mersenne_Prime_Search" title="Great Internet Mersenne Prime Search">GIMPS's</a> distributed <a href="/wiki/Mersenne_Prime" class="mw-redirect" title="Mersenne Prime">Mersenne Prime</a> search achieved about 0.313 PFLOPS through over 1.3 million computers.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.mersenne.org/primenet/">Internet PrimeNet Server</a> supports GIMPS's grid computing approach, one of the earliest and most successful<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> grid computing projects, since 1997.</p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quasi-opportunistic_approaches">Quasi-opportunistic approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Quasi-opportunistic approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Quasi-opportunistic_supercomputing" title="Quasi-opportunistic supercomputing">Quasi-opportunistic supercomputing</a></div>/n<p>Quasi-opportunistic supercomputing is a form of <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> whereby the â€œsuper virtual computerâ€ of many networked geographically disperse computers performs computing tasks that demand huge processing power.<sup id="cite_ref-Kravtsov_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kravtsov-84">[84]</a></sup> Quasi-opportunistic supercomputing aims to provide a higher quality of service than <a href="/wiki/Grid_computing" title="Grid computing">opportunistic grid computing</a> by achieving more control over the assignment of tasks to distributed resources and the use of intelligence about the availability and reliability of individual systems within the supercomputing network. However, quasi-opportunistic distributed execution of demanding parallel computing software in grids should be achieved through implementation of grid-wise allocation agreements, co-allocation subsystems, communication topology-aware allocation mechanisms, fault tolerant message passing libraries and data pre-conditioning.<sup id="cite_ref-Kravtsov_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kravtsov-84">[84]</a></sup></p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="HPC_in_the_Cloud">HPC in the Cloud</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: HPC in the Cloud">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<p><a href="/wiki/Cloud_computing" title="Cloud computing">Cloud Computing</a> with its recent and rapid expansions and development have grabbed the attention of HPC users and developers in recent years. Cloud Computing attempts to provide HPC-as-a-Service exactly like other forms of services currently available in the Cloud such as <a href="/wiki/Software_as_a_service" title="Software as a service">Software-as-a-Service</a>, <a href="/wiki/Platform_as_a_service" title="Platform as a service">Platform-as-a-Service</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure_as_a_service" class="mw-redirect" title="Infrastructure as a service">Infrastructure-as-a-Service</a>. HPC users may benefit from the Cloud in different angles such as scalability, resources being on-demand, fast, and inexpensive. On the other hand, moving HPC applications have a set of challenges too. Good examples of such challenges are <a href="/wiki/Virtualization" title="Virtualization">virtualization</a> overhead in the Cloud, multi-tenancy of resources, and network latency issues. Much research<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> is currently being done to overcome these challenges and make HPC in the cloud a more realistic possibility.</p>/n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Performance_measurement">Performance measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Performance measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capability_versus_capacity">Capability versus capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Capability versus capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<p>Supercomputers generally aim for the maximum in <i>capability computing</i> rather than <i>capacity computing</i>. Capability computing is typically thought of as using the maximum computing power to solve a single large problem in the shortest amount of time. Often a capability system is able to solve a problem of a size or complexity that no other computer can, e.g., a very complex <a href="/wiki/Weather_simulation" class="mw-redirect" title="Weather simulation">weather simulation</a> application.<sup id="cite_ref-NatCap_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatCap-89">[89]</a></sup></p>/n<p>Capacity computing, in contrast, is typically thought of as using efficient cost-effective computing power to solve a few somewhat large problems or many small problems.<sup id="cite_ref-NatCap_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatCap-89">[89]</a></sup> Architectures that lend themselves to supporting many users for routine everyday tasks may have a lot of capacity, but are not typically considered supercomputers, given that they do not solve a single very complex problem.<sup id="cite_ref-NatCap_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatCap-89">[89]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performance_metrics">Performance metrics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Performance metrics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/LINPACK_benchmarks" title="LINPACK benchmarks">LINPACK benchmarks</a></div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Supercomputing-rmax-graph2.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Supercomputing-rmax-graph2.svg/300px-Supercomputing-rmax-graph2.svg.png" width="300" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Supercomputing-rmax-graph2.svg/450px-Supercomputing-rmax-graph2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Supercomputing-rmax-graph2.svg/600px-Supercomputing-rmax-graph2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1260" data-file-height="810" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Supercomputing-rmax-graph2.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nTop supercomputer speeds: <i><a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">logscale</a> speed</i> over 60 years</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>In general, the speed of supercomputers is measured and <a href="/wiki/Benchmark_(computing)" title="Benchmark (computing)">benchmarked</a> in "<a href="/wiki/FLOPS" title="FLOPS">FLOPS</a>" (<i>FLoating point Operations Per Second</i>), and not in terms of "<a href="/wiki/Million_instructions_per_second" class="mw-redirect" title="Million instructions per second">MIPS</a>" (Million Instructions Per Second), as is the case with general-purpose computers.<sup id="cite_ref-Xifu_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Xifu-90">[90]</a></sup> These measurements are commonly used with an <a href="/wiki/SI_prefix" class="mw-redirect" title="SI prefix">SI prefix</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tera-" title="Tera-">tera-</a>, combined into the shorthand "TFLOPS" (10<sup>12</sup> FLOPS, pronounced <i>teraflops</i>), or <a href="/wiki/Peta-" title="Peta-">peta-</a>, combined into the shorthand "PFLOPS" (10<sup>15</sup> FLOPS, pronounced <i>petaflops</i>.) "<a href="/wiki/Petascale" class="mw-redirect" title="Petascale">Petascale</a>" supercomputers can process one quadrillion (10<sup>15</sup>) (1000 trillion) FLOPS. <a href="/wiki/Exascale_computing" title="Exascale computing">Exascale</a> is computing performance in the exaFLOPS (EFLOPS) range. An EFLOPS is one quintillion (10<sup>18</sup>) FLOPS (one million TFLOPS).</p>/n<p>No single number can reflect the overall performance of a computer system, yet the goal of the Linpack benchmark is to approximate how fast the computer solves numerical problems and it is widely used in the industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackpaper_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackpaper-91">[91]</a></sup> The FLOPS measurement is either quoted based on the theoretical floating point performance of a processor (derived from manufacturer's processor specifications and shown as "Rpeak" in the TOP500 lists) which is generally unachievable when running real workloads, or the achievable throughput, derived from the <a href="/wiki/LINPACK_benchmarks" title="LINPACK benchmarks">LINPACK benchmarks</a> and shown as "Rmax" in the TOP500 list. The LINPACK benchmark typically performs <a href="/wiki/LU_decomposition" title="LU decomposition">LU decomposition</a> of a large matrix. The LINPACK performance gives some indication of performance for some real-world problems, but does not necessarily match the processing requirements of many other supercomputer workloads, which for example may require more memory bandwidth, or may require better integer computing performance, or may need a high performance I/O system to achieve high levels of performance.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackpaper_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackpaper-91">[91]</a></sup></p>/n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_TOP500_list">The TOP500 list</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supercomputer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: The TOP500 list">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/TOP500" title="TOP500">TOP500</a></div>/n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_supercomputing" title="History of supercomputing">History of supercomputing</a></div>/n<div class="thumb tright">/n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Supercomputer_Share_Top500_November2015.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Supercomputer_Share_Top500_November2015.png/350px-Supercomputer_Share_Top500_November2015.png" width="350" height="303" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Supercomputer_Share_Top500_November2015.png/525px-Supercomputer_Share_Top500_November2015.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Supercomputer_Share_Top500_November2015.png/700px-Supercomputer_Share_Top500_November2015.png 2x" data-file-width="1302" data-file-height="1126" /></a>/n<div class="thumbcaption">/n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Supercomputer_Share_Top500_November2015.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>/nDistribution of TOP500 supercomputers among different countries, as of November 2015</div>/n</div>/n</div>/n<p>Since 1993, the fastest supercomputers have been ranked on the TOP500 list according to their <a href="/wiki/LINPACK_benchmarks" title="LINPACK benchmarks">LINPACK benchmark</a> results. The list does not claim to be unbiased or definitive, but it is a widely cited current definition of the "fastest" supercomputer available at any given time.</p>/n<p>This is a recent list of the computers which appeared at the top of the TOP500 list,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> and the "Peak speed" is given as the "Rmax" rating.</p>/n<div
